class="mediawiki ltr sitedir-ltr mw-hide-empty-elt ns-0 ns-subject mw-editable page-Meteoroid rootpage-Meteoroid skin-vector action-view"> <div class="noprint" id="mw-page-base"></div>\n<div class="noprint" id="mw-head-base"></div>\n<div class="mw-body" id="content" role="main">\n<a id="top"></a>\n<div class="mw-body-content" id="siteNotice"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Meteoroid</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Meteor&amp;redirect=no" title="Meteor">Meteor</a>)</span></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">Meteorite</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meteoroid_meteor_meteorite.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="445" data-file-width="597" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Meteoroid_meteor_meteorite.gif/330px-Meteoroid_meteor_meteorite.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Meteoroid_meteor_meteorite.gif/495px-Meteoroid_meteor_meteorite.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/63/Meteoroid_meteor_meteorite.gif 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meteoroid_meteor_meteorite.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <i>meteoroid</i> shown entering the atmosphere, becoming visible as a <i>meteor</i> and hitting the Earth\'s surface as a <i>meteorite</i>.</div></div></div>\n<p>A <b>meteoroid</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'m\' in \'my\'">m</span><span title="/i&#720;/: \'ee\' in \'fleece\'">i&#720;</span><span title="\'t\' in \'tie\'">t</span><span title="/i/: \'y\' in \'happy\'">i</span><span title="/&#601;/: \'a\' in \'about\'">&#601;</span><span title="\'r\' in \'rye\'">r</span><span title="/&#596;&#618;/: \'oi\' in \'choice\'">&#596;&#618;</span><span title="\'d\' in \'dye\'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> is a small rocky or metallic body in <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a>.\n</p><p>Meteoroids are significantly smaller than <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>, and range in size from small grains to one-meter-wide objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin2010_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin2010-2">[2]</a></sup> Objects smaller than this are classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrometeoroids" title="Micrometeoroids">micrometeoroids</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_dust" title="Space dust">space dust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin2010_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin2010-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-difference_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-difference-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Most are fragments from <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a> or asteroids, whereas others are <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">collision impact</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">debris</a> ejected from bodies such as the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When a meteoroid, comet, or asteroid <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">enters Earth\'s atmosphere</a> at a speed typically in excess of 20&#160;km/s (72,000&#160;km/h; 45,000&#160;mph), <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamic_heating" title="Aerodynamic heating">aerodynamic heating</a> of that object produces a streak of light, both from the glowing object and the trail of glowing particles that it leaves in its wake. This phenomenon is called a <a href="#Meteors">meteor</a> or "shooting star". A series of many meteors appearing seconds or minutes apart and appearing to originate from the same fixed point in the sky is called a <a href="#Meteor_shower">meteor shower</a>. If that object withstands <a href="/wiki/Ablation" title="Ablation">ablation</a> from its passage through the atmosphere as a meteor and impacts with the ground, it is then called a <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorite</a>.\n</p><p>An estimated 25 million meteoroids, micrometeoroids and other <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">space debris</a> enter Earth\'s atmosphere each day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> which results in an estimated 15,000 tonnes of that material entering the atmosphere each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Relating to meteoroids</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Relating_to_meteors"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span meteorites</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Meteoroids">Meteoroids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Meteoroids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Micrometeoroid" title="Micrometeoroid">Micrometeoroid</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meteoroid_track_through_aerogel_from_EURECA_mission.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="690" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Meteoroid_track_through_aerogel_from_EURECA_mission.jpg/220px-Meteoroid_track_through_aerogel_from_EURECA_mission.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Meteoroid_track_through_aerogel_from_EURECA_mission.jpg/330px-Meteoroid_track_through_aerogel_from_EURECA_mission.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Meteoroid_track_through_aerogel_from_EURECA_mission.jpg/440px-Meteoroid_track_through_aerogel_from_EURECA_mission.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meteoroid_track_through_aerogel_from_EURECA_mission.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Meteoroid embedded in <a href="/wiki/Aerogel" title="Aerogel">aerogel</a>; the meteoroid is 10&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9Cm" title="&#924;m">&#181;m</a> in diameter and its track is 1.5&#160;mm long</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:323213main_Petersmeteorites_946-710.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="710" data-file-width="946" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/323213main_Petersmeteorites_946-710.jpg/220px-323213main_Petersmeteorites_946-710.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/323213main_Petersmeteorites_946-710.jpg/330px-323213main_Petersmeteorites_946-710.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/323213main_Petersmeteorites_946-710.jpg/440px-323213main_Petersmeteorites_946-710.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:323213main_Petersmeteorites_946-710.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2008_TC3" title="2008 TC3">2008 TC<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">3</span></span></a></span> <a href="#Meteorite">meteorite</a> fragments found on February 28, 2009, in the <a href="/wiki/Nubian_Desert" title="Nubian Desert">Nubian Desert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1961, the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU) defined a meteoroid as "a solid object moving in interplanetary space, of a size considerably smaller than an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> and considerably larger than an atom".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMO-meteoroid_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMO-meteoroid-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Millman1961_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Millman1961-11">[11]</a></sup> In 1995, Beech and Steel, writing in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quarterly_Journal_of_the_Royal_Astronomical_Society" title="Quarterly Journal of the Royal Astronomical Society">Quarterly Journal of the Royal Astronomical Society</a></i>, proposed a new definition where a meteoroid would be between 100 <a href="/wiki/Micrometer" title="Micrometer">&#181;m</a> and 10&#160;m (33&#160;ft) across.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steel1995_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steel1995-12">[12]</a></sup> In 2010, following the discovery of asteroids below 10 m in size, Rubin and Grossman proposed a revision of the previous definition of meteoroid to objects between 10&#160;&#181;m and one meter (3&#160;ft 3&#160;in) in diameter in order to maintain the distinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin2010_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin2010-2">[2]</a></sup> According to Rubin and Grossman, the minimum size of an asteroid is given by what can be discovered from Earth-bound telescopes, so the distinction between meteoroid and asteroid is fuzzy. Some of the smallest asteroids discovered (based on <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude#Solar_System_bodies_(H)" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> <i>H</i>) are <span class="nowrap">2008 TS<sub>26</sub></span> with <i>H</i> = 33.2<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smallest-jpl_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-smallest-jpl-13">[13]</a></sup> and <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2011_CQ1" title="2011 CQ1">2011 CQ<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">1</span></span></a></span> with <i>H</i> = 32.1<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011CQ1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-2011CQ1-14">[14]</a></sup> both with an estimated size of one&#160;m (3&#160;ft 3&#160;in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-166_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-166-15">[15]</a></sup> In April 2017, the IAU adopted an official revision of its definition, limiting size to between 30&#160;&#181;m and one meter in diameter, but allowing for a deviation for any object causing a meteor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-met-def-2017_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-met-def-2017-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Objects smaller than meteoroids are classified as <a href="/wiki/Micrometeoroid" title="Micrometeoroid">micrometeoroids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_dust_cloud" title="Interplanetary dust cloud">interplanetary dust</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">Minor Planet Center</a> does not use the term "meteoroid".\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Almost all meteoroids contain extraterrestrial nickel and iron. They have three main classifications: iron, stone, and stony-iron. Some stone meteoroids contain grain-like inclusions known as <a href="/wiki/Chondrule" title="Chondrule">chondrules</a> and are called <a href="/wiki/Chondrite" title="Chondrite">chondrites</a>. Stony meteoroids without these features are called "<a href="/wiki/Achondrite" title="Achondrite">achondrites</a>", which are typically formed from extraterrestrial igneous activity; they contain little or no extraterrestrial iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The composition of meteoroids can be inferred as they pass through Earth\'s atmosphere from their trajectories and the light spectra of the resulting meteor. Their effects on radio signals also give information, especially useful for daytime meteors, which are otherwise very difficult to observe. From these trajectory measurements, meteoroids have been found to have many different orbits, some clustering in streams <i>(see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteor_showers" title="Meteor showers">meteor showers</a>)</i> often associated with a parent <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a>, others apparently sporadic. Debris from meteoroid streams may eventually be scattered into other orbits. The light spectra, combined with trajectory and light curve measurements, have yielded various compositions and densities, ranging from fragile snowball-like objects with density about a quarter that of ice,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USRA-1183_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-USRA-1183-18">[18]</a></sup> to nickel-iron rich dense rocks. The study of <a href="/wiki/Meteorite#Meteorite_types" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> also gives insights into the composition of non-ephemeral meteoroids.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_Solar_System">In the Solar System</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: In the Solar System">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Most meteoroids come from the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, having been perturbed by the gravitational influences of planets, but others are particles from <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>, giving rise to <a href="/wiki/Meteor_shower" title="Meteor shower">meteor showers</a>. Some meteoroids are fragments from bodies such as Mars or <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">our moon</a>, that have been thrown into space by an impact.\n</p><p>Meteoroids travel around the Sun in a variety of orbits and at various velocities. The fastest move at about 42&#160;km/s (94,000&#160;mph) through space in the vicinity of Earth\'s orbit. This is <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a> from the Sun, equal to the square root of two times Earth\'s speed, and is the upper speed limit of objects in the vicinity of Earth, unless they come from interstellar space. Earth travels at about 29.6&#160;km/s (66,000&#160;mph), so when meteoroids meet the atmosphere head-on (which only occurs when meteors are in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" title="Retrograde orbit">retrograde orbit</a> such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eta_Aquarids" title="Eta Aquarids">Eta Aquarids</a>, which are associated with the retrograde Halley\'s Comet) the combined speed may reach about 71&#160;km/s (160,000&#160;mph) (see <a href="/wiki/Specific_energy#Astrodynamics" title="Specific energy">Specific energy#Astrodynamics</a>). Meteoroids moving through Earth\'s orbital space average about 20&#160;km/s (45,000&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-2060_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-2060-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On January 17, 2013 at 05:21 PST, a one meter-sized comet from the <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a> entered Earth atmosphere over <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAMS_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-CAMS-20">[20]</a></sup> The object had a retrograde orbit with perihelion at 0.98&#160;&#177;&#160;0.03&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>. It approached from the direction of the constellation <a href="/wiki/Virgo_(constellation)" title="Virgo (constellation)">Virgo</a> (which was in the south about 50&#176; above the horizon at the time), and collided head-on with Earth\'s atmosphere at 72&#160;&#177;&#160;6&#160;km/s (161,000&#160;&#177;&#160;13,000&#160;mph)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAMS_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-CAMS-20">[20]</a></sup> vapourising more than 100&#160;km (330,000&#160;ft) above ground over a period of several seconds.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Collision_with_Earth.27s_atmosphere"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Collision_with_Earth\'s_atmosphere">Collision with Earth\'s atmosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Collision with Earth\'s atmosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>When meteoroids intersect with Earth\'s atmosphere at night, they are likely to become visible as <a href="#Meteors">meteors</a>. If meteoroids survive the entry through the atmosphere and reach Earth\'s surface, they are called <a href="#Meteorites">meteorites</a>. Meteorites are transformed in structure and chemistry by the heat of entry and force of impact. A noted 4-metre (13&#160;ft) <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2008_TC3" title="2008 TC3">2008 TC<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">3</span></span></a></span>, was observed in space on a collision course with Earth on 6&#160;October 2008 and entered Earth\'s atmosphere the next day, striking a remote area of northern Sudan. It was the first time that a meteoroid had been observed in space and tracked prior to impacting Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMO-meteoroid_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-IMO-meteoroid-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> has produced a map showing the most notable asteroid collisions with Earth and its atmosphere from 1994 to 2013 from data gathered by U.S. government sensors (see below).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Meteors"><span id="Meteor"></span> Meteors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Meteors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Meteor" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Meteor_(disambiguation)" title="Meteor (disambiguation)">Meteor (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cosmic_Fireball_Falling_Over_ALMA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Cosmic_Fireball_Falling_Over_ALMA.jpg/220px-Cosmic_Fireball_Falling_Over_ALMA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Cosmic_Fireball_Falling_Over_ALMA.jpg/330px-Cosmic_Fireball_Falling_Over_ALMA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Cosmic_Fireball_Falling_Over_ALMA.jpg/440px-Cosmic_Fireball_Falling_Over_ALMA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cosmic_Fireball_Falling_Over_ALMA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Meteor seen from the site of the <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Large_Millimeter_Array" title="Atacama Large Millimeter Array">Atacama Large Millimeter Array</a> (ALMA)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1064" data-file-width="1519" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg/220px-SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg/330px-SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg/440px-SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World map of large meteoric events <i>(also see <a href="#Fireball">Fireball</a> below)</i>&#160;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>A <b>meteor</b>, known colloquially as a <b><dfn>shooting star</dfn></b> or <b><dfn>falling star</dfn></b>, is the visible passage of a glowing <a href="#Meteoroids">meteoroid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Micrometeoroid" title="Micrometeoroid">micrometeoroid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> or <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> through Earth\'s atmosphere, after being heated to incandescence by collisions with air molecules in the upper atmosphere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMO-meteoroid_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-IMO-meteoroid-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M-W_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-M-W-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> creating a streak of light via its rapid motion and sometimes also by shedding glowing material in its wake. Although a meteor may seem to be a few thousand feet from the Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> meteors typically occur in the <a href="/wiki/Mesosphere" title="Mesosphere">mesosphere</a> at altitudes from 76 to 100&#160;km (250,000 to 330,000&#160;ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIT-pje_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-pje-26">[26]</a></sup> The root word <i>meteor</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <i>mete&#333;ros</i>, meaning "high in the air".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M-W_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-M-W-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Millions of meteors occur in Earth\'s atmosphere daily. Most meteoroids that cause meteors are about the size of a grain of sand, i.e. they are usually millimeter-sized or smaller. Meteoroid sizes can be calculated from their mass and density which, in turn, can be estimated from the observed meteor trajectory in the upper atmosphere.\n<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJ-18_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJ-18-27">[27]</a></sup>\nMeteors may occur in <a href="/wiki/Meteor_shower" title="Meteor shower">showers</a>, which arise when Earth passes through a stream of debris left by a comet, or as "random" or "sporadic" meteors, not associated with a specific stream of <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">space debris</a>. A number of specific meteors have been observed, largely by members of the public and largely by accident, but with enough detail that orbits of the meteoroids producing the meteors have been calculated. The atmospheric velocities of meteors result from the movement of Earth around the Sun at about 30&#160;km/s (67,000&#160;mph),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-earthfact_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-earthfact-28">[28]</a></sup> the orbital speeds of meteoroids, and the <a href="/wiki/Gravity_well" title="Gravity well">gravity well</a> of Earth.\n</p><p>Meteors become visible between about 75 to 120&#160;km (250,000 to 390,000&#160;ft) above Earth. They usually disintegrate at altitudes of 50 to 95&#160;km (160,000 to 310,000&#160;ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteorsandtheirparentcomets_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-meteorsandtheirparentcomets-29">[29]</a></sup> Meteors have roughly a fifty percent chance of a daylight (or&#160;near daylight) collision with Earth. Most meteors are, however, observed at night, when darkness allows fainter objects to be recognized. For bodies with a size scale larger than 10&#160;cm (3.9&#160;in) to several meters meteor visibility is due to the atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Ram_pressure" title="Ram pressure">ram pressure</a> (not friction) that heats the meteoroid so that it glows and creates a shining trail of gases and melted meteoroid particles. The gases include vaporised meteoroid material and atmospheric gases that heat up when the meteoroid passes through the atmosphere. Most meteors glow for about a second.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Although meteors have been known since ancient times, they were not known to be an astronomical phenomenon until early in the nineteenth century. Prior to that, they were seen in the West as an atmospheric phenomenon, like lightning, and were not connected with strange stories of rocks falling from the sky. In 1807, <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> chemistry professor <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Silliman" title="Benjamin Silliman">Benjamin Silliman</a> investigated a meteorite that fell in <a href="/wiki/Weston_meteorite" title="Weston meteorite">Weston, Connecticut</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jefferson_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-jefferson-30">[30]</a></sup> Silliman believed the meteor had a cosmic origin, but meteors did not attract much attention from astronomers until the spectacular meteor storm of November 1833.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1833leonids_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-1833leonids-31">[31]</a></sup> People all across the eastern United States saw thousands of meteors, radiating from a single point in the sky. Astute observers noticed that <a href="/wiki/Radiant_(meteor_shower)" title="Radiant (meteor shower)">the radiant</a>, as the point is now called, moved with the stars, staying in the constellation Leo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJSA-1834_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJSA-1834-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The astronomer <a href="/wiki/Denison_Olmsted" title="Denison Olmsted">Denison Olmsted</a> made an extensive study of this storm, and concluded that it had a cosmic origin. After reviewing historical records, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Wilhelm_Matthias_Olbers" title="Heinrich Wilhelm Matthias Olbers">Heinrich Wilhelm Matthias Olbers</a> predicted the storm\'s return in 1867, which drew the attention of other astronomers to the phenomenon. <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Anson_Newton" title="Hubert Anson Newton">Hubert A. Newton</a>\'s more thorough historical work led to a refined prediction of 1866, which proved to be correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1833leonids_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-1833leonids-31">[31]</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Schiaparelli" title="Giovanni Schiaparelli">Giovanni Schiaparelli</a>\'s success in connecting the <a href="/wiki/Leonids" title="Leonids">Leonids</a> (as they are now called) with comet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tempel-Tuttle" title="Tempel-Tuttle">Tempel-Tuttle</a>, the cosmic origin of meteors was now firmly established. Still, they remain an atmospheric phenomenon, and retain their name "meteor" from the Greek word for "atmospheric".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-318_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-318-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fireball">Fireball</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Fireball">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_bolides" title="List of bolides">List of bolides</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e6/%D0%92%D0%B7%D1%80%D1%8B%D0%B2_%D0%BC%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%B4_%D0%A7%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%B1%D0%B8%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BC_15_02_2013_avi-iCawTYPtehk.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%92%D0%B7%D1%80%D1%8B%D0%B2_%D0%BC%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0%D0%B4_%D0%A7%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%B1%D0%B8%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BC_15_02_2013_avi-iCawTYPtehk.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Footage of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superbolide" title="Superbolide">superbolide</a>, a very bright fireball that <a href="/wiki/Chelyabinsk_meteor" title="Chelyabinsk meteor">exploded over Chelyabinsk</a> Oblast, Russia in 2013</div></div></div>\n<p>A <b>fireball</b> is a brighter-than-usual meteor. The <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU) defines a fireball as "a meteor brighter than any of the planets" (<a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> &#8722;4 or greater).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meteor-13871_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-meteor-13871-34">[34]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Meteor_Organization" title="International Meteor Organization">International Meteor Organization</a> (an amateur organization that studies meteors) has a more rigid definition. It defines a fireball as a meteor that would have a magnitude of &#8722;3 or brighter if seen at <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">zenith</a>. This definition corrects for the greater distance between an observer and a meteor near the horizon. For example, a meteor of magnitude &#8722;1 at 5 degrees above the horizon would be classified as a fireball because, if the observer had been directly below the meteor, it would have appeared as magnitude &#8722;6.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMO-fireball_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMO-fireball-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fireballs reaching apparent magnitude &#8722;14 or brighter are called <a href="/wiki/Bolide" title="Bolide">bolides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belton_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belton-36">[36]</a></sup> The IAU has no official definition of "bolide", and generally considers the term synonymous with "fireball". Astronomers often use "bolide" to identify an exceptionally bright fireball, particularly one that explodes. They are sometimes called detonating fireballs <i>(also see <a href="/wiki/List_of_meteor_air_bursts" title="List of meteor air bursts">List of meteor air bursts</a>)</i>. It may also be used to mean a fireball which creates audible sounds. In the late twentieth century, bolide has also come to mean any object that hits Earth and explodes, with no regard to its composition (asteroid or comet).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Google-Book-jR84AAAAIAAJ_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Google-Book-jR84AAAAIAAJ-37">[37]</a></sup> The word <i>bolide</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> &#946;&#959;&#955;&#943;&#962; (<i>bolis</i>) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-myet-bolide_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-myet-bolide-38">[38]</a></sup> which can mean <i>a missile</i> or <i>to flash</i>. If the magnitude of a bolide reaches &#8722;17 or brighter it is known as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superbolide" title="Superbolide">superbolide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belton_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Belton-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Google-Book-3Hd8Lw1hExUC_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Google-Book-3Hd8Lw1hExUC-39">[39]</a></sup> A relatively small percentage of fireballs hit Earth\'s atmosphere and then pass out again: these are termed <a href="/wiki/Earth-grazing_fireball" title="Earth-grazing fireball">Earth-grazing fireballs</a>. Such an event happened in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Great_Daylight_1972_Fireball" title="The Great Daylight 1972 Fireball">broad daylight over North America in 1972</a>. Another rare phenomena is a <a href="/wiki/Meteor_procession" title="Meteor procession">meteor procession</a>, where the meteor breaks up into several fireballs traveling nearly parallel to the surface of Earth.\n</p><p>A steadily growing number of fireballs are recorded at the <a href="/wiki/American_Meteor_Society" title="American Meteor Society">American Meteor Society</a> every year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMS-Fireballs_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMS-Fireballs-40">[40]</a></sup> There are probably more than 500,000 fireballs a year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMS-FAQ_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMS-FAQ-41">[41]</a></sup> but most will go unnoticed because most will occur over the ocean and half will occur during daytime.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center; margin: 0.1em auto;">\n<caption>Fireball Sightings reported to the American Meteor Society&#8201;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMS-Fireballs_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-AMS-Fireballs-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Year\n</th>\n<th width="55">2008\n</th>\n<th width="55">2009\n</th>\n<th width="55">2010\n</th>\n<th width="55">2011\n</th>\n<th width="55">2012\n</th>\n<th width="55">2013\n</th>\n<th width="55">2014\n</th>\n<th width="55">2015\n</th>\n<th width="55">2016\n</th>\n<th width="55">2017\n</th>\n<th width="55">2018\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Number</b></td>\n<td>724</td>\n<td>668</td>\n<td>941</td>\n<td>1,653</td>\n<td>2,172</td>\n<td>3,556</td>\n<td>3,778</td>\n<td>4,233</td>\n<td>5,371</td>\n<td>5,470</td>\n<td><i>4,301</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_atmosphere">Effect on atmosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Effect on atmosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Ionization trail" and "Dark flight (astronomy)" redirect here. For the movie, see <a href="/wiki/Dark_Flight" title="Dark Flight">Dark Flight</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Perseid_meteor-trail_Corona_Borealis0.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="675" data-file-width="675" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Perseid_meteor-trail_Corona_Borealis0.jpg/220px-Perseid_meteor-trail_Corona_Borealis0.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Perseid_meteor-trail_Corona_Borealis0.jpg/330px-Perseid_meteor-trail_Corona_Borealis0.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Perseid_meteor-trail_Corona_Borealis0.jpg/440px-Perseid_meteor-trail_Corona_Borealis0.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Perseid_meteor-trail_Corona_Borealis0.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ionization trail of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perseid_meteor" title="Perseid meteor">Perseid meteor</a> seen against the constellation <a href="/wiki/Corona_Borealis" title="Corona Borealis">Corona Borealis</a> with its ring of stars</div></div></div>\n<p>The entry of meteoroids into Earth\'s atmosphere produces three main effects: ionization of atmospheric molecules, dust that the meteoroid sheds, and the sound of passage. During the entry of a meteoroid or asteroid into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_atmosphere" title="Upper atmosphere">upper atmosphere</a>, an <b>ionization trail</b> is created, where the air molecules are <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionized</a> by the passage of the meteor. Such ionization trails can last up to 45 minutes at a time.\n</p><p>Small, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sand-grain" title="Sand-grain">sand-grain</a> sized meteoroids are entering the atmosphere constantly, essentially every few seconds in any given region of the atmosphere, and thus ionization trails can be found in the upper atmosphere more or less continuously. When radio waves are bounced off these trails, it is called <a href="/wiki/Meteor_burst_communications" title="Meteor burst communications">meteor burst communications</a>. Meteor radars can measure atmospheric density and winds by measuring the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decay_rate" title="Decay rate">decay rate</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shift</a> of a meteor trail. Most meteoroids burn up when they enter the atmosphere. The left-over debris is called <i>meteoric dust</i> or just meteor dust. Meteor dust particles can persist in the atmosphere for up to several months. These particles might affect climate, both by scattering electromagnetic radiation and by catalyzing chemical reactions in the upper atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-443141a_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-443141a-43">[43]</a></sup> Meteoroids or their fragments may achieve <b>dark flight</b> after deceleration to <a href="/wiki/Terminal_velocity" title="Terminal velocity">terminal velocity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMO-meteorites_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMO-meteorites-44">[44]</a></sup> Dark flight starts when they decelerate to about 2&#8211;4&#160;km/s (4,500&#8211;8,900&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMS-Fireball_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMS-Fireball-45">[45]</a></sup> Larger fragments will fall further down the <a href="/wiki/Strewn_field" title="Strewn field">strewn field</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Colours">Colours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Colours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leonid_Meteor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3054" data-file-width="2988" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Leonid_Meteor.jpg/220px-Leonid_Meteor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Leonid_Meteor.jpg/330px-Leonid_Meteor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Leonid_Meteor.jpg/440px-Leonid_Meteor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leonid_Meteor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A meteor of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leonid_meteor_shower" title="Leonid meteor shower">Leonid meteor shower</a>, the photograph shows the meteor, afterglow, and wake as distinct components</div></div></div>\n<p>The visible light produced by a meteor may take on various hues, depending on the chemical composition of the meteoroid, and the speed of its movement through the atmosphere. As layers of the meteoroid abrade and ionize, the colour of the light emitted may change according to the layering of minerals. Colours of meteors depend on the relative influence of the metallic content of the meteoroid versus the superheated air plasma, which its passage engenders:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Orange-yellow (<a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>)</li>\n<li>Yellow (<a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>)</li>\n<li>Blue-green (<a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>)</li>\n<li>Violet (<a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>)</li>\n<li>Red (atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>)</li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Acoustic_manifestations">Acoustic manifestations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Acoustic manifestations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Sound generated by a meteor in the upper atmosphere, such as a <a href="/wiki/Sonic_boom" title="Sonic boom">sonic boom</a>, typically arrives many seconds after the visual light from a meteor disappears. Occasionally, as with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leonid_meteor_shower" title="Leonid meteor shower">Leonid meteor shower</a> of 2001, "crackling", "swishing", or "hissing" sounds have been reported,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-find-87854873_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-find-87854873-47">[47]</a></sup> occurring at the same instant as a meteor flare. Similar sounds have also been reported during intense displays of Earth\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">auroras</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auroral-sounds_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-auroral-sounds-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auroral-acoustics_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-auroral-acoustics-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AG16-155_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-AG16-155-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JRASC-373K_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-JRASC-373K-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Theories on the generation of these sounds may partially explain them. For example, scientists at NASA suggested that the turbulent ionized <a href="/wiki/Wake" title="Wake">wake</a> of a meteor interacts with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_field" title="Geomagnetic field">Earth\'s magnetic field</a>, generating pulses of <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a>. As the trail dissipates, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">megawatts</a> of electromagnetic power could be released, with a peak in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_spectrum" title="Power spectrum">power spectrum</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Audio_frequencies" title="Audio frequencies">audio frequencies</a>. Physical <a href="/wiki/Vibration" title="Vibration">vibrations</a> induced by the electromagnetic impulses would then be heard if they are powerful enough to make grasses, plants, eyeglass frames, and other conductive materials vibrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-ast26_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-ast26-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> This proposed mechanism, although proven to be plausible by laboratory work, remains unsupported by corresponding measurements in the field. Sound recordings made under controlled conditions in Mongolia in 1998 support the contention that the sounds are real.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-321596_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-321596-56">[56]</a></sup> <i>(Also see <a href="/wiki/Bolide" title="Bolide">Bolide</a>.)</i>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meteor_shower">Meteor shower</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Meteor shower">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Meteor_shower" title="Meteor shower">Meteor shower</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_meteor_showers" title="List of meteor showers">List of meteor showers</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meteor_burst.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1422" data-file-width="1422" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Meteor_burst.jpg/220px-Meteor_burst.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Meteor_burst.jpg/330px-Meteor_burst.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Meteor_burst.jpg/440px-Meteor_burst.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meteor_burst.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Multiple meteors photographed over an extended <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exposure_time" title="Exposure time">exposure time</a> during a <a href="/wiki/Meteor_shower" title="Meteor shower">meteor shower</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1178" data-file-width="1419" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg/220px-PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg/330px-PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg/440px-PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PSM_V18_D201_Shower_of_perseids_sept_6_and_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Meteor_shower" title="Meteor shower">Meteor shower</a> on chart</div></div></div>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Meteor_shower" title="Meteor shower">meteor shower</a> is the result of an interaction between a planet, such as Earth, and streams of debris from a <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> or other source. The passage of Earth through cosmic debris from comets and other sources is a <a href="/wiki/List_of_meteor_showers" title="List of meteor showers">recurring event</a> in many cases. Comets can produce debris by water vapor drag, as demonstrated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fred_Whipple" title="Fred Whipple">Fred Whipple</a> in 1951,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvard-464W_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harvard-464W-57">[57]</a></sup> and by breakup. Each time a comet swings by the Sun in its <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a>, some of its ice vaporizes and a certain amount of meteoroids will be shed. The meteoroids spread out along the entire orbit of the comet to form a meteoroid stream, also known as a "dust trail" (as opposed to a comet\'s "dust tail" caused by the very small particles that are quickly blown away by solar radiation pressure).\n</p><p>The frequency of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fireball_(meteor)" title="Fireball (meteor)">fireball</a> sightings increases by about 10-30% during the weeks of <a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">vernal equinox</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fireball_season_NASA_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-fireball_season_NASA-58">[58]</a></sup> Even <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorite</a> falls are more common during the northern hemisphere\'s spring season. Although this phenomenon has been known for quite some time, the reason behind the anomaly is not fully understood by scientists. Some researchers attribute this to an intrinsic variation in the meteoroid population along Earth\'s orbit, with a peak in big fireball-producing debris around spring and early summer. Others have pointed out that during this period the ecliptic is (in the northern hemisphere) high in the sky in the late afternoon and early evening. This means that fireball radiants with an asteroidal source are high in the sky (facilitating relatively high rates) at the moment the meteoroids "catch up" with Earth, coming from behind going in the same direction as Earth. This causes relatively low relative speeds and from this low entry speeds, which facilitates survival of meteorites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> It also generates high fireball rates in the early evening, increasing chances of eye witness reports. This explains a part, but perhaps not all of the seasonal variation. Research is in progress for mapping the orbits of the meteors to gain a better understanding of the phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-camera_network_NASA_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-camera_network_NASA-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_meteors">Notable meteors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Notable meteors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object#Notable_objects" title="Near-Earth object">Near-Earth object &#167;&#160;Notable objects</a></div>\n<dl><dt>1992&#8212;Peekskill, New York</dt></dl>\n<dl><dd>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peekskill_Meteorite" title="Peekskill Meteorite">Peekskill Meteorite</a> was filmed on October&#160;9, 1992 by at least 16 independent videographers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uwo-peeks_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-uwo-peeks-61">[61]</a></sup> Eyewitness accounts indicate the fireball entry of the Peekskill meteorite started over West Virginia at 23:48 UT (&#177;1&#160;min). The fireball, which traveled in a northeasterly direction, had a pronounced greenish colour, and attained an estimated peak visual magnitude of &#8722;13. During a luminous flight time that exceeded 40 seconds the fireball covered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_path" title="Ground path">ground path</a> of some 430 to 500&#160;mi (700 to 800&#160;km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-6464_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-6464-62">[62]</a></sup> One meteorite recovered at <a href="/wiki/Peekskill,_New_York" title="Peekskill, New York">Peekskill, New York</a>, for which the event and object gained their name, had a mass of 27&#160;lb (12.4&#160;kg) and was subsequently identified as an H6 monomict breccia meteorite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MB-75_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-MB-75-63">[63]</a></sup> The video record suggests that the Peekskill meteorite had several companions over a wide area. The companions are unlikely to be recovered in the hilly, wooded terrain in the vicinity of Peekskill.</dd></dl>\n<dl><dt>2009&#8212;Bone, Indonesia</dt></dl>\n<dl><dd>A large fireball was observed in the skies near Bone, Indonesia on October&#160;8, 2009. This was thought to be caused by an asteroid approximately 10&#160;m (33&#160;ft) in diameter. The fireball contained an estimated energy of 50 kilotons of TNT, or about twice the <a href="/wiki/Fat_Man" title="Fat Man">Nagasaki atomic bomb</a>. No injuries were reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEO165_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-NEO165-64">[64]</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<dl><dt>2009&#8212;Southwestern US</dt></dl>\n<dl><dd>A large bolide was reported on 18 November 2009 over southeastern California, northern Arizona, Utah, Wyoming, Idaho and Colorado. At 00:07 local time a security camera at the high altitude W. L. Eccles Observatory (9,610&#160;ft (2,930&#160;m) above sea level) recorded a movie of the passage of the object to the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YT-XiL1UGbHXSI_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-YT-XiL1UGbHXSI-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YT-kE109bKQESw_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-YT-kE109bKQESw-66">[66]</a></sup> Of particular note in this video is the spherical "ghost" image slightly trailing the main object (this is likely a lens reflection of the intense fireball), and the bright fireball explosion associated with the breakup of a substantial fraction of the object. An object trail can be seen to continue northward after the bright fireball event. The shock from the final breakup triggered seven seismological stations in northern Utah; a timing fit to the seismic data yielded a terminal location of the object at 40.286 N, -113.191 W, altitude 90,000&#160;ft (27&#160;km).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This is above the Dugway Proving Grounds, a closed Army testing base.</dd></dl>\n<dl><dt>2013&#8212;Chelyabinsk Oblast, Russia</dt></dl>\n<dl><dd>The <a href="/wiki/Chelyabinsk_meteor" title="Chelyabinsk meteor">Chelyabinsk meteor</a> was an extremely bright, exploding <a href="#fireball">fireball</a>, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superbolide" title="Superbolide">superbolide</a>, measuring about 17 to 20&#160;m (56 to 66&#160;ft) across, with an estimated initial mass of 11,000 tonnes, as the relatively small <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> entered Earth\'s atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPL20130301_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPL20130301-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-061_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-061-68">[68]</a></sup> It was the largest known natural object to have entered Earth\'s atmosphere since the <a href="/wiki/Tunguska_event" title="Tunguska event">Tunguska event</a> in 1908. Over 1,500 people were injured mostly by glass from shattered windows caused by the <a href="/wiki/Air_burst" title="Air burst">air burst</a> approximately 25 to 30&#160;km (80,000 to 100,000&#160;ft) above the <a href="/wiki/Chelyabinsk_Oblast" title="Chelyabinsk Oblast">environs of Chelyabinsk</a>, Russia on 15 February 2013. An increasingly bright streak was observed during morning daylight with a large contrail lingering behind. At no less than 1 minute and up to at least 3 minutes after the object peaked in intensity (depending on distance from trail), a large concussive blast was heard that shattered windows and set-off car alarms, which was followed by a number of smaller explosions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian-20130215_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-20130215-69">[69]</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallery_of_meteors">Gallery of meteors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a width="407"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker%E2%80%93Levy_9" title="Comet Shoemaker&#8211;Levy 9">Comet Shoemaker&#8211;Levy 9</a> colliding with Jupiter: The sequence shows fragment W turning into a <a href="#Fireball">fireball</a> on the planet\'s dark side\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Meteorites">Meteorites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Meteorites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">Meteorite</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Murnpeowie_meteorite.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="715" data-file-width="929" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Murnpeowie_meteorite.jpg/220px-Murnpeowie_meteorite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Murnpeowie_meteorite.jpg/330px-Murnpeowie_meteorite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Murnpeowie_meteorite.jpg/440px-Murnpeowie_meteorite.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Murnpeowie_meteorite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Murnpeowie <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorite</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Iron_meteorite" title="Iron meteorite">iron meteorite</a> with <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/regmaglypt" title="wikt:regmaglypt">regmaglypts</a> resembling thumbprints (Australia, 1910)</div></div></div>\n<p>A meteorite is a portion of a meteoroid or asteroid that survives its passage through the atmosphere and hits the ground without being destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-70">[70]</a></sup> Meteorites are sometimes, but not always, found in association with hypervelocity <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impact craters</a>; during energetic collisions, the entire impactor may be vaporized, leaving no meteorites. <a href="/wiki/Geologist" title="Geologist">Geologists</a> use the term, "bolide", in a different sense from <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomers</a> to indicate a very large <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">impactor</a>. For example, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">USGS</a> uses the term to mean a generic large crater-forming projectile in a manner "to imply that we do not know the precise nature of the impacting body ... whether it is a rocky or metallic asteroid, or an icy comet for example".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS-bolide_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-bolide-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Meteoroids also hit other bodies in the Solar System. On such stony bodies as the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> that have little or no atmosphere, they leave enduring craters.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Frequency_of_impacts">Frequency of impacts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Frequency of impacts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object#Planet_Earth_collision_probability_with_near-Earth_objects" title="Near-Earth object">Planet Earth collision probability with near-Earth objects</a></div>\n<p>The diameter of the largest impactor to hit Earth on any given day is likely to be about 40 centimeters (16 inches), in a given year about four metres (13&#160;ft), and in a given century about 20&#160;m (66&#160;ft). These statistics are obtained by the following:\n</p><p>Over at least the range from five centimeters (2.0 inches) to roughly 300 meters (980 feet), the rate at which Earth receives meteors obeys a <a href="/wiki/Power_law" title="Power law">power-law</a> distribution as follows:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle N(&gt;D)=37D^{-2.7}\\ }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>N</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mo>&gt;</mo>\n<mi>D</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>37</mn>\n<msup>\n<mi>D</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>2.7</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mtext>&#160;</mtext>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle N(&gt;D)=37D^{-2.7}\\ }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="N(&gt;D) = 37 D^{-2.7}\\ " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/140c6bd0be862423cbd7096cc5a711fbbca60142" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:19.791ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <i>N</i> (&gt;<i>D</i>) is the expected number of objects larger than a diameter of <i>D</i> meters to hit Earth in a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-01238_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-01238-72">[72]</a></sup> This is based on observations of bright meteors seen from the ground and space, combined with surveys of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroids" title="Near-Earth asteroids">near-Earth asteroids</a>. Above 300&#160;m (980&#160;ft) in diameter, the predicted rate is somewhat higher, with a two kilometres (one point two miles) asteroid (one million-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megatons" title="Megatons">megaton</a> TNT equivalent) every couple of million years &#8212; about 10 times as often as the power-law extrapolation would predict.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_craters">Impact craters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Impact craters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">Impact crater</a></div>\n<p>Meteoroid collisions with solid Solar System objects, including the Moon, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, and most small moons and <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>, create impact craters, which are the dominant geographic features of many of those objects. On other planets and moons with active surface geological processes, such as Earth, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, visible impact craters may become <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">eroded</a>, buried, or transformed by <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">tectonics</a> over time. In early literature, before the significance of impact cratering was widely recognised, the terms <a href="/wiki/Cryptoexplosion" title="Cryptoexplosion">cryptoexplosion</a> or cryptovolcanic structure were often used to describe what are now recognised as impact-related features on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USRA-954_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-USRA-954-73">[73]</a></sup> Molten terrestrial material ejected from a meteorite impact crater can cool and solidify into an object known as a <a href="/wiki/Tektite" title="Tektite">tektite</a>. These are often mistaken for meteorites.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallery_of_meteorites">Gallery of meteorites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meteoroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Gallery of meteorites">edit</a><span
